<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word "photography" was created from the Greek roots φωτός (<i>phōtós</i>), genitive of φῶς (<i>phōs</i>), "light"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and γραφή (<i>graphé</i>) "representation by means of lines" or "drawing",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> together meaning "drawing with light".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several people may have coined the same new term from these roots independently. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hercules_Florence" title="Hercules Florence">Hercules Florence</a>, a French painter and inventor living in Campinas, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, used the French form of the word, <i>photographie</i>, in private notes which a Brazilian historian believes were written in 1834.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> This claim is widely reported but is not yet largely recognized internationally. The first use of the word by the Franco-Brazilian inventor became widely known after the research of Boris Kossoy in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The German newspaper <i>Vossische Zeitung</i> of 25 February 1839 contained an article entitled <i>Photographie</i>, discussing several priority claims – especially <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fox_Talbot" title="Henry Fox Talbot">Henry Fox Talbot</a>'s – regarding Daguerre's claim of invention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The article is the earliest known occurrence of the word in public print.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> It was signed "J.M.", believed to have been Berlin astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johann_von_Maedler" title="Johann von Maedler">Johann von Maedler</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eder_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eder-9">[9]</a></sup> The astronomer Sir <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">John Herschel</a> is also credited with coining the word, independent of Talbot, in 1839.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The inventors <a href="/wiki/Nic%C3%A9phore_Ni%C3%A9pce" title="Nicéphore Niépce">Nicéphore Niépce</a>, Henry Fox Talbot, and <a href="/wiki/Louis_Daguerre" title="Louis Daguerre">Louis Daguerre</a> seem not to have known or used the word "photography", but referred to their processes as "Heliography" (Niépce), "Photogenic Drawing"/"Talbotype"/"Calotype" (Talbot), and "Daguerreotype" (Daguerre).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eder_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eder-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_photography" title="History of photography">History of photography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_photography_technology" title="Timeline of photography technology">Timeline of photography technology</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_camera" title="History of the camera">History of the camera</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precursor_technologies">Precursor technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Precursor technologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Camera_obscura_box.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Camera_obscura_box.jpg/220px-Camera_obscura_box.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Camera_obscura_box.jpg/330px-Camera_obscura_box.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Camera_obscura_box.jpg/440px-Camera_obscura_box.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Camera_obscura_box.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A camera obscura used for drawing</div></div></div>
<p>Photography is the result of combining several technical discoveries, relating to seeing an image and capturing the image. The discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Camera_obscura" title="Camera obscura">camera obscura</a> ("dark chamber" in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>) that provides an image of a scene dates back to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Science_and_Technology_in_China" title="History of Science and Technology in China">ancient China</a>. Greek mathematicians <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> independently described a camera obscura in the 5th and 4th centuries BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krebs_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krebs-12">[12]</a></sup> In the 6th century CE, Byzantine mathematician <a href="/wiki/Anthemius_of_Tralles" title="Anthemius of Tralles">Anthemius of Tralles</a> used a type of camera obscura in his experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Physics_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Physics in the medieval Islamic world">Arab physicist</a> <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a> (Alhazen) (965–1040) also invented a camera obscura as well as the first true <a href="/wiki/Pinhole_camera" title="Pinhole camera">pinhole camera</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krebs_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Krebs-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plott_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plott-15">[15]</a></sup> The invention of the camera has been traced back to the work of Ibn al-Haytham.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belbachir_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belbachir-16">[16]</a></sup> While the effects of a single light passing through a pinhole had been described earlier,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belbachir_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Belbachir-16">[16]</a></sup> Ibn al-Haytham gave the first correct analysis of the camera obscura,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> including the first geometrical and quantitative descriptions of the phenomenon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> and was the first to use a screen in a dark room so that an image from one side of a hole in the surface could be projected onto a screen on the other side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> He also first understood the relationship between the <a href="/wiki/Focus_(optics)" title="Focus (optics)">focal point</a> and the pinhole,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and performed early experiments with <a href="/wiki/Afterimage" title="Afterimage">afterimages</a>, laying the foundations for the invention of photography in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plott_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plott-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> mentions natural camerae obscurae that are formed by dark caves on the edge of a sunlit valley. A hole in the cave wall will act as a pinhole camera and project a laterally reversed, upside down image on a piece of paper. <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> painters used the camera obscura which, in fact, gives the optical rendering in color that dominates Western Art. It is a box with a small hole in one side, which allows specific light rays to enter, projecting an inverted image onto a viewing screen or paper.
</p><p>The birth of photography was then concerned with inventing means to capture and keep the image produced by the camera obscura. <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a> (1193–1280) discovered <a href="/wiki/Silver_nitrate" title="Silver nitrate">silver nitrate</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Georg_Fabricius" title="Georg Fabricius">Georg Fabricius</a> (1516–1571) discovered <a href="/wiki/Silver_chloride" title="Silver chloride">silver chloride</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and the techniques described in <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Optics" title="Book of Optics">Book of Optics</a> are capable of producing primitive photographs using medieval materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Daniele_Barbaro" title="Daniele Barbaro">Daniele Barbaro</a> described a <a href="/wiki/Diaphragm_(optics)" title="Diaphragm (optics)">diaphragm</a> in 1566.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gernsheim_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gernsheim-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Homberg" title="Wilhelm Homberg">Wilhelm Homberg</a> described how light darkened some chemicals (photochemical effect) in 1694.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The fiction book <i><a href="/wiki/Giphantie" title="Giphantie">Giphantie</a></i>, published in 1760, by French author <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tiphaigne_de_la_Roche" title="Tiphaigne de la Roche">Tiphaigne de la Roche</a>, described what can be interpreted as photography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gernsheim_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gernsheim-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around the year 1800, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">British</a> inventor <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wedgwood_(photographer)" title="Thomas Wedgwood (photographer)">Thomas Wedgwood</a> made the first known attempt to capture the image in a camera obscura by means of a light-sensitive substance. He used paper or white leather treated with <a href="/wiki/Silver_nitrate" title="Silver nitrate">silver nitrate</a>. Although he succeeded in capturing the shadows of objects placed on the surface in direct sunlight, and even made shadow copies of paintings on glass, it was reported in 1802 that "the images formed by means of a camera obscura have been found too faint to produce, in any moderate time, an effect upon the nitrate of silver." The shadow images eventually darkened all over.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invention">Invention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Invention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nic%C3%A9phore_Ni%C3%A9pce_Oldest_Photograph_1825.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="990" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Nic%C3%A9phore_Ni%C3%A9pce_Oldest_Photograph_1825.jpg/240px-Nic%C3%A9phore_Ni%C3%A9pce_Oldest_Photograph_1825.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Nic%C3%A9phore_Ni%C3%A9pce_Oldest_Photograph_1825.jpg/360px-Nic%C3%A9phore_Ni%C3%A9pce_Oldest_Photograph_1825.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Nic%C3%A9phore_Ni%C3%A9pce_Oldest_Photograph_1825.jpg/480px-Nic%C3%A9phore_Ni%C3%A9pce_Oldest_Photograph_1825.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nic%C3%A9phore_Ni%C3%A9pce_Oldest_Photograph_1825.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Earliest known surviving heliographic engraving, 1825, printed from a metal plate made by <a href="/wiki/Nic%C3%A9phore_Ni%C3%A9pce" title="Nicéphore Niépce">Nicéphore Niépce</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UTexas_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-UTexas-28">[28]</a></sup> The plate was exposed under an ordinary engraving and copied it by photographic means. This was a step towards the first permanent photograph taken with a camera.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:398px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:View_from_the_Window_at_Le_Gras_colorized_2020_new.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2618" data-file-width="6394" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/View_from_the_Window_at_Le_Gras_colorized_2020_new.png/396px-View_from_the_Window_at_Le_Gras_colorized_2020_new.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/View_from_the_Window_at_Le_Gras_colorized_2020_new.png/594px-View_from_the_Window_at_Le_Gras_colorized_2020_new.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/View_from_the_Window_at_Le_Gras_colorized_2020_new.png/792px-View_from_the_Window_at_Le_Gras_colorized_2020_new.png 2x" width="396"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:View_from_the_Window_at_Le_Gras_colorized_2020_new.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/View_from_the_Window_at_Le_Gras" title="View from the Window at Le Gras">View from the Window at Le Gras</a></i>, 1826 or 1827, the earliest surviving camera photograph. Original plate (left) and <a href="/wiki/Film_colorization" title="Film colorization">colorized</a> reoriented enhancement (right).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gezicht_op_de_Predikherenlei_en_-brug_te_Gent,_ca._1839,_STAM_Gent.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3835" data-file-width="5165" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Gezicht_op_de_Predikherenlei_en_-brug_te_Gent%2C_ca._1839%2C_STAM_Gent.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Gezicht_op_de_Predikherenlei_en_-brug_te_Gent%2C_ca._1839%2C_STAM_Gent.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Gezicht_op_de_Predikherenlei_en_-brug_te_Gent%2C_ca._1839%2C_STAM_Gent.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Gezicht_op_de_Predikherenlei_en_-brug_te_Gent%2C_ca._1839%2C_STAM_Gent.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Gezicht_op_de_Predikherenlei_en_-brug_te_Gent%2C_ca._1839%2C_STAM_Gent.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Gezicht_op_de_Predikherenlei_en_-brug_te_Gent%2C_ca._1839%2C_STAM_Gent.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gezicht_op_de_Predikherenlei_en_-brug_te_Gent,_ca._1839,_STAM_Gent.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of the Predikherenlei en Predikherenbrug  in <a href="/wiki/Ghent" title="Ghent">Ghent</a>, October 1839, collection STAM - <a href="/wiki/Ghent_City_Museum" title="Ghent City Museum">Ghent City Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>The first permanent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photoetching" title="Photoetching">photoetching</a> was an image produced in 1822 by the French inventor <a href="/wiki/Nic%C3%A9phore_Ni%C3%A9pce" title="Nicéphore Niépce">Nicéphore Niépce</a>, but it was destroyed in a later attempt to make prints from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UTexas_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-UTexas-28">[28]</a></sup> Niépce was successful again in 1825. In 1826 or 1827, he made the <i><a href="/wiki/View_from_the_Window_at_Le_Gras" title="View from the Window at Le Gras">View from the Window at Le Gras</a></i>, the earliest surviving photograph from nature (i.e., of the image of a real-world scene, as formed in a <a href="/wiki/Camera_obscura" title="Camera obscura">camera obscura</a> by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">lens</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boulevard_du_Temple_by_Daguerre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2472" data-file-width="3441" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Boulevard_du_Temple_by_Daguerre.jpg/220px-Boulevard_du_Temple_by_Daguerre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Boulevard_du_Temple_by_Daguerre.jpg/330px-Boulevard_du_Temple_by_Daguerre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Boulevard_du_Temple_by_Daguerre.jpg/440px-Boulevard_du_Temple_by_Daguerre.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boulevard_du_Temple_by_Daguerre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/View_of_the_Boulevard_du_Temple" title="View of the Boulevard du Temple">View of the Boulevard du Temple</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Daguerreotype" title="Daguerreotype">daguerreotype</a> made by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Daguerre" title="Louis Daguerre">Louis Daguerre</a> in 1838, is generally accepted as the earliest photograph to include people. It is a view of a busy street, but because the exposure lasted for several minutes the moving traffic left no trace. Only the two men near the bottom left corner, one of them apparently having his boots polished by the other, remained in one place long enough to be visible.</div></div></div>
<p>Because Niépce's camera photographs required an extremely long <a href="/wiki/Exposure_(photography)" title="Exposure (photography)">exposure</a> (at least eight hours and probably several days), he sought to greatly improve his <a href="/wiki/Bitumen_of_Judea" title="Bitumen of Judea">bitumen</a> process or replace it with one that was more practical. In partnership with <a href="/wiki/Louis_Daguerre" title="Louis Daguerre">Louis Daguerre</a>, he worked out post-exposure processing methods that produced visually superior results and replaced the bitumen with a more light-sensitive resin, but hours of exposure in the camera were still required. With an eye to eventual commercial exploitation, the partners opted for total secrecy.
</p><p>Niépce died in 1833 and Daguerre then redirected the experiments toward the light-sensitive <a href="/wiki/Silver_halide" title="Silver halide">silver halides</a>, which Niépce had abandoned many years earlier because of his inability to make the images he captured with them light-fast and permanent. Daguerre's efforts culminated in what would later be named the <a href="/wiki/Daguerreotype" title="Daguerreotype">daguerreotype</a> process. The essential elements—a silver-plated surface sensitized by <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a> vapor, developed by <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> vapor, and "fixed" with hot saturated <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">salt</a> water—were in place in 1837. The required exposure time was measured in minutes instead of hours. Daguerre took the earliest confirmed photograph of a person in 1838 while capturing a view of a Paris street: unlike the other pedestrian and horse-drawn traffic on the busy boulevard, which appears deserted, one man having his boots polished stood sufficiently still throughout the several-minutes-long exposure to be visible. The existence of Daguerre's process was publicly announced, without details, on 7 January 1839. The news created an international sensation. France soon agreed to pay Daguerre a pension in exchange for the right to present his invention to the world as the gift of France, which occurred when complete working instructions were unveiled on 19 August 1839. In that same year, American photographer <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cornelius" title="Robert Cornelius">Robert Cornelius</a> is credited with taking the earliest surviving photographic self-portrait.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Latticed_window_at_lacock_abbey_1835.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="638" data-file-width="489" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Latticed_window_at_lacock_abbey_1835.jpg/170px-Latticed_window_at_lacock_abbey_1835.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Latticed_window_at_lacock_abbey_1835.jpg/255px-Latticed_window_at_lacock_abbey_1835.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Latticed_window_at_lacock_abbey_1835.jpg/340px-Latticed_window_at_lacock_abbey_1835.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Latticed_window_at_lacock_abbey_1835.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A latticed window in <a href="/wiki/Lacock_Abbey" title="Lacock Abbey">Lacock Abbey</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, photographed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Fox_Talbot" title="William Fox Talbot">William Fox Talbot</a> in 1835. Shown here in positive form, this may be the oldest extant photographic negative made in a camera.</div></div></div>
<p>In Brazil, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hercules_Florence" title="Hercules Florence">Hercules Florence</a> had apparently started working out a silver-salt-based paper process in 1832, later naming it <i>Photographie</i>.
</p><p>Meanwhile, a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">British</a> inventor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Fox_Talbot" title="William Fox Talbot">William Fox Talbot</a>, had succeeded in making crude but reasonably light-fast silver images on paper as early as 1834 but had kept his work secret. After reading about Daguerre's invention in January 1839, Talbot published his hitherto secret method and set about improving on it. At first, like other pre-daguerreotype processes, Talbot's paper-based photography typically required hours-long <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exposures" title="Exposures">exposures</a> in the camera, but in 1840 he created the <a href="/wiki/Calotype" title="Calotype">calotype</a> process, which used the <a href="/wiki/Photographic_processing" title="Photographic processing">chemical development</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Latent_image" title="Latent image">latent image</a> to greatly reduce the exposure needed and compete with the daguerreotype. In both its original and calotype forms, Talbot's process, unlike Daguerre's, created a translucent <a href="/wiki/Negative_(photography)" title="Negative (photography)">negative</a> which could be used to print multiple positive copies; this is the basis of most modern chemical photography up to the present day, as daguerreotypes could only be replicated by rephotographing them with a camera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Talbot's famous tiny paper negative of the Oriel window in <a href="/wiki/Lacock_Abbey" title="Lacock Abbey">Lacock Abbey</a>, one of a number of camera photographs he made in the summer of 1835, may be the oldest camera negative in existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>In France, <a href="/wiki/Hippolyte_Bayard" title="Hippolyte Bayard">Hippolyte Bayard</a> invented his own process for producing direct positive paper prints and claimed to have invented photography earlier than Daguerre or Talbot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>British chemist <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">John Herschel</a> made many contributions to the new field. He invented the <a href="/wiki/Cyanotype" title="Cyanotype">cyanotype</a> process, later familiar as the "blueprint". He was the first to use the terms "photography", "negative" and "positive". He had discovered in 1819 that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sodium_thiosulphate" title="Sodium thiosulphate">sodium thiosulphate</a> was a solvent of silver halides, and in 1839 he informed Talbot (and, indirectly, Daguerre) that it could be used to "fix" silver-halide-based photographs and make them completely light-fast. He made the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glass_negative" title="Glass negative">glass negative</a> in late 1839.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wilson_Chinn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="735" decoding="async" height="359" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Wilson_Chinn.jpg/220px-Wilson_Chinn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Wilson_Chinn.jpg/330px-Wilson_Chinn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Wilson_Chinn.jpg/440px-Wilson_Chinn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wilson_Chinn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wilson_Chinn" title="Wilson Chinn">Wilson Chinn</a>, a branded slave from Louisiana--per <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a>, "one of the earliest and most dramatic examples of how the newborn medium of photography could change the course of history."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/w/index.php?title=File:The_Macon_city_directory,_embracing_a_full_alphabetical_record_of_the_names_and_inhabitants_of_Macon_and_its_suburbs._A_business_directory_of_the_city;_county_and_city_governments;_societies,_a_-_DPLA_-_f06f279fd02ed401f5126198836a5269.pdf&amp;page=3"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1577" data-file-width="981" decoding="async" height="354" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/The_Macon_city_directory%2C_embracing_a_full_alphabetical_record_of_the_names_and_inhabitants_of_Macon_and_its_suburbs._A_business_directory_of_the_city%3B_county_and_city_governments%3B_societies%2C_a_-_DPLA_-_f06f279fd02ed401f5126198836a5269.pdf/page3-220px-thumbnail.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/The_Macon_city_directory%2C_embracing_a_full_alphabetical_record_of_the_names_and_inhabitants_of_Macon_and_its_suburbs._A_business_directory_of_the_city%3B_county_and_city_governments%3B_societies%2C_a_-_DPLA_-_f06f279fd02ed401f5126198836a5269.pdf/page3-330px-thumbnail.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/The_Macon_city_directory%2C_embracing_a_full_alphabetical_record_of_the_names_and_inhabitants_of_Macon_and_its_suburbs._A_business_directory_of_the_city%3B_county_and_city_governments%3B_societies%2C_a_-_DPLA_-_f06f279fd02ed401f5126198836a5269.pdf/page3-440px-thumbnail.pdf.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Macon_city_directory,_embracing_a_full_alphabetical_record_of_the_names_and_inhabitants_of_Macon_and_its_suburbs._A_business_directory_of_the_city;_county_and_city_governments;_societies,_a_-_DPLA_-_f06f279fd02ed401f5126198836a5269.pdf?page=3" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Advertisement for Campbell's Photograph Gallery from The Macon City Directory, circa 1877.</div></div></div>
<p>In the March 1851 issue of <i>The Chemist</i>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Scott_Archer" title="Frederick Scott Archer">Frederick Scott Archer</a> published his wet plate <a href="/wiki/Collodion_process" title="Collodion process">collodion process</a>. It became the most widely used photographic medium until the gelatin dry plate, introduced in the 1870s, eventually replaced it. There are three subsets to the collodion process; the <a href="/wiki/Ambrotype" title="Ambrotype">Ambrotype</a> (a positive image on glass), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrotype" title="Ferrotype">Ferrotype</a> or Tintype (a positive image on metal) and the glass negative, which was used to make positive prints on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albumen" title="Albumen">albumen</a> or salted paper.
</p><p>Many advances in <a href="/wiki/Photographic_plate" title="Photographic plate">photographic glass plates</a> and printing were made during the rest of the 19th century. In 1891, <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Lippmann" title="Gabriel Lippmann">Gabriel Lippmann</a> introduced a process for making natural-color photographs based on the optical phenomenon of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference</a> of light waves. His scientifically elegant and important but ultimately impractical invention earned him the Nobel Prize in Physics in 1908.
</p><p>Glass plates were the medium for most original camera photography from the late 1850s until the general introduction of flexible plastic films during the 1890s. Although the convenience of the film greatly popularized amateur photography, early films were somewhat more expensive and of markedly lower optical quality than their glass plate equivalents, and until the late 1910s they were not available in the large formats preferred by most professional photographers, so the new medium did not immediately or completely replace the old. Because of the superior dimensional stability of glass, the use of plates for some scientific applications, such as <a href="/wiki/Astrophotography" title="Astrophotography">astrophotography</a>, continued into the 1990s, and in the niche field of laser <a href="/wiki/Holography" title="Holography">holography</a>, it has persisted into the 21st century.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film">Film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Photographic_film" title="Photographic film">Photographic film</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Undeveloped_film.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="181" data-file-width="494" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Undeveloped_film.png/350px-Undeveloped_film.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/89/Undeveloped_film.png 1.5x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Undeveloped_film.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Undeveloped Arista black-and-white film, <a href="/wiki/Film_speed" title="Film speed">ISO</a> 125/22°</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hurter_and_Driffield" title="Hurter and Driffield">Hurter and Driffield</a> began pioneering work on the <a href="/wiki/Sensitometry" title="Sensitometry">light sensitivity</a> of photographic emulsions in 1876. Their work enabled the first quantitative measure of film speed to be devised.
</p><p>The first flexible photographic roll film was marketed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastman_Kodak" title="Eastman Kodak">George Eastman</a>, founder of <a href="/wiki/Kodak" title="Kodak">Kodak</a> in 1885, but this original "film" was actually a coating on a paper base. As part of the processing, the image-bearing layer was stripped from the paper and transferred to a hardened gelatin support. The first transparent plastic roll film followed in 1889. It was made from highly flammable <a href="/wiki/Nitrocellulose#Film" title="Nitrocellulose">nitrocellulose</a> known as nitrate film.
</p><p>Although <a href="/wiki/Cellulose_acetate" title="Cellulose acetate">cellulose acetate</a> or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safety_film" title="Safety film">safety film</a>" had been introduced by Kodak in 1908,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> at first it found only a few special applications as an alternative to the hazardous nitrate film, which had the advantages of being considerably tougher, slightly more transparent, and cheaper. The changeover was not completed for <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> films until 1933, and although safety film was always used for 16 mm and 8 mm home movies, nitrate film remained standard for theatrical 35 mm motion pictures until it was finally discontinued in 1951.
</p><p>Films remained the dominant form of photography until the early 21st century when advances in digital photography drew consumers to digital formats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Although modern photography is dominated by digital users, film continues to be used by enthusiasts and professional photographers. The distinctive "look" of film based photographs compared to digital images is likely due to a combination of factors, including (1) differences in spectral and tonal sensitivity (S-shaped density-to-exposure (H&amp;D curve) with film vs. linear response curve for digital CCD sensors),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> (2) resolution, and (3) continuity of tone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Black-and-white">Black-and-white</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Black-and-white">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Monochrome_photography" title="Monochrome photography">Monochrome photography</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black-and-White" title="Black-and-White">Black-and-White</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dark_room.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2933" data-file-width="4414" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Dark_room.jpg/220px-Dark_room.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Dark_room.jpg/330px-Dark_room.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Dark_room.jpg/440px-Dark_room.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dark_room.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A photographic <a href="/wiki/Darkroom" title="Darkroom">darkroom</a> with <a href="/wiki/Safelight" title="Safelight">safelight</a></div></div></div>
<p>Originally, all photography was <a href="/wiki/Monochrome_photography" title="Monochrome photography">monochrome</a>, or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black-and-white" title="Black-and-white">black-and-white</a></i>. Even after color film was readily available, black-and-white photography continued to dominate for decades, due to its lower cost, chemical stability, and its "classic" photographic look. The tones and contrast between light and dark areas define black-and-white photography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Monochromatic pictures are not necessarily composed of pure blacks, whites, and intermediate shades of gray but can involve shades of one particular <a href="/wiki/Hue" title="Hue">hue</a> depending on the process. The <a href="/wiki/Cyanotype" title="Cyanotype">cyanotype</a> process, for example, produces an image composed of blue tones. The <a href="/wiki/Albumen_print" title="Albumen print">albumen print</a> process, publicly revealed in 1847, produces brownish tones.
</p><p>Many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photographers" title="Photographers">photographers</a> continue to produce some monochrome images, sometimes because of the established archival permanence of well-processed silver-halide-based materials. Some full-color digital images are processed using a variety of techniques to create black-and-white results, and some manufacturers produce digital cameras that exclusively shoot monochrome. Monochrome printing or electronic display can be used to salvage certain photographs taken in color which are unsatisfactory in their original form; sometimes when presented as black-and-white or single-color-toned images they are found to be more effective. Although color photography has long predominated, monochrome images are still produced, mostly for artistic reasons. Almost all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_cameras" title="Digital cameras">digital cameras</a> have an option to shoot in monochrome, and almost all image editing software can combine or selectively discard <a href="/wiki/RGB_color_model" title="RGB color model">RGB</a> color channels to produce a monochrome image from one shot in color.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Color">Color</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Color">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Color_photography" title="Color photography">Color photography</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tartan_Ribbon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Tartan_Ribbon.jpg/220px-Tartan_Ribbon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Tartan_Ribbon.jpg/330px-Tartan_Ribbon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Tartan_Ribbon.jpg/440px-Tartan_Ribbon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tartan_Ribbon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Color_photograph" title="Color photograph">color photograph</a> made by the three-color method suggested by <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> in 1855, taken in 1861 by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sutton_(photographer)" title="Thomas Sutton (photographer)">Thomas Sutton</a>. The subject is a colored, <a href="/wiki/Tartan" title="Tartan">tartan</a> patterned ribbon.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Color_photography" title="Color photography">Color photography</a> was explored beginning in the 1840s. Early experiments in color required extremely long exposures (hours or days for camera images) and could not "fix" the photograph to prevent the color from quickly fading when exposed to white light.
</p><p>The first permanent color photograph was taken in 1861 using the three-color-separation principle first published by Scottish physicist <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> in 1855.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King's_College_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-King's_College-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maxwell_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maxwell-41">[41]</a></sup> The foundation of virtually all practical color processes, Maxwell's idea was to take three separate black-and-white photographs through red, green and blue <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filter_(photography)" title="Filter (photography)">filters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King's_College_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-King's_College-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maxwell_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Maxwell-41">[41]</a></sup> This provides the photographer with the three basic channels required to recreate a color image. Transparent prints of the images could be projected through similar color filters and superimposed on the projection screen, an <a href="/wiki/Additive_color" title="Additive color">additive method</a> of color reproduction. A color print on paper could be produced by superimposing <a href="/wiki/Carbon_print" title="Carbon print">carbon prints</a> of the three images made in their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complementary_color" title="Complementary color">complementary colors</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Subtractive_color" title="Subtractive color">subtractive method</a> of color reproduction pioneered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_Ducos_du_Hauron" title="Louis Ducos du Hauron">Louis Ducos du Hauron</a> in the late 1860s.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colonel_William_Willoughby_Verner,_Sanger_Shepherd_process,_by_Sarah_Acland_1903.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="347" data-file-width="301" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Colonel_William_Willoughby_Verner%2C_Sanger_Shepherd_process%2C_by_Sarah_Acland_1903.png/220px-Colonel_William_Willoughby_Verner%2C_Sanger_Shepherd_process%2C_by_Sarah_Acland_1903.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b6/Colonel_William_Willoughby_Verner%2C_Sanger_Shepherd_process%2C_by_Sarah_Acland_1903.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colonel_William_Willoughby_Verner,_Sanger_Shepherd_process,_by_Sarah_Acland_1903.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Color photography was possible long before <a href="/wiki/Kodachrome" title="Kodachrome">Kodachrome</a>, as this 1903 portrait by <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Angelina_Acland" title="Sarah Angelina Acland">Sarah Angelina Acland</a> demonstrates, but in its earliest years, the need for special equipment, long exposures, and complicated printing processes made it extremely rare.</div></div></div>
<p>Russian photographer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sergei_Mikhailovich_Prokudin-Gorskii" title="Sergei Mikhailovich Prokudin-Gorskii">Sergei Mikhailovich Prokudin-Gorskii</a> made extensive use of this color separation technique, employing a special camera which successively exposed the three color-filtered images on different parts of an oblong <a href="/wiki/Photographic_plate" title="Photographic plate">plate</a>. Because his exposures were not simultaneous, unsteady subjects exhibited color "fringes" or, if rapidly moving through the scene, appeared as brightly colored ghosts in the resulting projected or printed images.
</p><p>Implementation of color photography was hindered by the limited sensitivity of early photographic materials, which were mostly sensitive to blue, only slightly sensitive to green, and virtually insensitive to red. The discovery of dye sensitization by photochemist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermann_W._Vogel" title="Hermann W. Vogel">Hermann Vogel</a> in 1873 suddenly made it possible to add sensitivity to green, yellow and even red. Improved color sensitizers and ongoing improvements in the overall sensitivity of <a href="/wiki/Photographic_emulsion" title="Photographic emulsion">emulsions</a> steadily reduced the once-prohibitive long exposure times required for color, bringing it ever closer to commercial viability.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autochrome" title="Autochrome">Autochrome</a>, the first commercially successful color process, was introduced by the <a href="/wiki/Auguste_and_Louis_Lumi%C3%A8re" title="Auguste and Louis Lumière">Lumière brothers</a> in 1907. Autochrome <a href="/wiki/Photographic_plate" title="Photographic plate">plates</a> incorporated a <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> color filter layer made of dyed grains of <a href="/wiki/Potato_starch" title="Potato starch">potato starch</a>, which allowed the three color components to be recorded as adjacent microscopic image fragments. After an Autochrome plate was <a href="/wiki/Reversal_film" title="Reversal film">reversal processed</a> to produce a positive <a href="/wiki/Reversal_film" title="Reversal film">transparency</a>, the starch grains served to illuminate each fragment with the correct color and the tiny colored points blended together in the eye, synthesizing the color of the subject by the <a href="/wiki/Additive_color" title="Additive color">additive method</a>. Autochrome plates were one of several varieties of additive color screen plates and films marketed between the 1890s and the 1950s.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kodachrome" title="Kodachrome">Kodachrome</a>, the first modern "integral tripack" (or "monopack") color film, was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Kodak" title="Kodak">Kodak</a> in 1935. It captured the three color components in a multi-layer <a href="/wiki/Photographic_emulsion" title="Photographic emulsion">emulsion</a>. One layer was sensitized to record the red-dominated part of the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">spectrum</a>, another layer recorded only the green part and a third recorded only the blue. Without special <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Film_processing" title="Film processing">film processing</a>, the result would simply be three superimposed black-and-white images, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complementary_color" title="Complementary color">complementary</a> cyan, magenta, and yellow dye images were created in those layers by adding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Color_coupler" title="Color coupler">color couplers</a> during a complex processing procedure.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Agfa-Gevaert" title="Agfa-Gevaert">Agfa's</a> similarly structured <a href="/wiki/Agfacolor" title="Agfacolor">Agfacolor</a> Neu was introduced in 1936. Unlike Kodachrome, the color couplers in Agfacolor Neu were incorporated into the emulsion layers during manufacture, which greatly simplified the processing. Currently, available color films still employ a multi-layer emulsion and the same principles, most closely resembling Agfa's product.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Instant_film" title="Instant film">Instant color film</a>, used in a special camera which yielded a unique finished color print only a minute or two after the exposure, was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Polaroid_Corporation" title="Polaroid Corporation">Polaroid</a> in 1963.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Color_photography" title="Color photography">Color photography</a> may form images as positive transparencies, which can be used in a <a href="/wiki/Slide_projector" title="Slide projector">slide projector</a>, or as color negatives intended for use in creating positive color enlargements on specially coated paper. The latter is now the most common form of film (non-digital) color photography owing to the introduction of automated photo printing equipment. After a transition period centered around 1995–2005, color film was relegated to a niche market by inexpensive multi-megapixel digital cameras. Film continues to be the preference of some photographers because of its distinctive "look".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digital">Digital</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Digital">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Digital_photography" title="Digital photography">Digital photography</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Digital_camera" title="Digital camera">Digital camera</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:153px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Early_digital!.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Early_digital%21.jpg/151px-Early_digital%21.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Early_digital%21.jpg/226px-Early_digital%21.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Early_digital%21.jpg/301px-Early_digital%21.jpg 2x" width="151"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Early_digital!.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kodak DCS 100, based on a <a href="/wiki/Nikon_F3" title="Nikon F3">Nikon F3</a> body with Digital Storage Unit</div></div></div>
<p>In 1981, <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a> unveiled the first consumer camera to use a <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">charge-coupled device</a> for imaging, eliminating the need for film: the <a href="/wiki/Sony_Mavica" title="Sony Mavica">Sony Mavica</a>. While the Mavica saved images to disk, the images were displayed on television, and the camera was not fully digital.
</p><p>The first digital camera to both record and save images in a digital format was the Fujix DS-1P created by Fujfilm in 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1991, Kodak unveiled the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DCS_100" title="DCS 100">DCS 100</a>, the first commercially available digital single lens reflex camera. Although its high cost precluded uses other than <a href="/wiki/Photojournalism" title="Photojournalism">photojournalism</a> and professional photography, commercial <a href="/wiki/Digital_photography" title="Digital photography">digital photography</a> was born.
</p><p>Digital imaging uses an electronic <a href="/wiki/Image_sensor" title="Image sensor">image sensor</a> to record the image as a set of electronic data rather than as chemical changes on film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> An important difference between digital and chemical photography is that chemical photography resists <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photo_manipulation" title="Photo manipulation">photo manipulation</a> because it involves <a href="/wiki/Photographic_film" title="Photographic film">film</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photographic_paper" title="Photographic paper">photographic paper</a>, while digital imaging is a highly manipulative medium. This difference allows for a degree of image post-processing that is comparatively difficult in film-based photography and permits different communicative potentials and applications.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Smartphone_photography.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2988" data-file-width="5312" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Smartphone_photography.jpg/220px-Smartphone_photography.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Smartphone_photography.jpg/330px-Smartphone_photography.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Smartphone_photography.jpg/440px-Smartphone_photography.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Smartphone_photography.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photography on a smartphone</div></div></div>
<p>Digital photography dominates the 21st century. More than 99% of photographs taken around the world are through digital cameras, increasingly through smartphones.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Techniques">Techniques</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Techniques">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chicago-Calder-Diagonal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1465" data-file-width="2376" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Chicago-Calder-Diagonal.jpg/220px-Chicago-Calder-Diagonal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Chicago-Calder-Diagonal.jpg/330px-Chicago-Calder-Diagonal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Chicago-Calder-Diagonal.jpg/440px-Chicago-Calder-Diagonal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chicago-Calder-Diagonal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Angles such as vertical, horizontal, or as pictured here diagonal are considered important photographic techniques</div></div></div>
<p>A large variety of photographic techniques and media are used in the process of capturing images for photography. These include the camera; dualphotography; full-spectrum, ultraviolet and infrared media; light field photography; and other imaging techniques.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cameras">Cameras</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cameras">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Camera" title="Camera">Camera</a></div>
<p>The camera is the image-forming device, and a <a href="/wiki/Photographic_plate" title="Photographic plate">photographic plate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photographic_film" title="Photographic film">photographic film</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> electronic <a href="/wiki/Image_sensor" title="Image sensor">image sensor</a> is the capture medium. The respective recording medium can be the plate or film itself, or a digital magnetic or electronic memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Photographers control the camera and lens to "expose" the light recording material to the required amount of light to form a "<a href="/wiki/Latent_image" title="Latent image">latent image</a>" (on plate or film) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RAW_file" title="RAW file">RAW file</a> (in digital cameras) which, after appropriate processing, is converted to a usable image. <a href="/wiki/Digital_photography" title="Digital photography">Digital cameras</a> use an electronic image sensor based on light-sensitive electronics such as <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">charge-coupled device</a> (CCD) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complementary_metal-oxide-semiconductor" title="Complementary metal-oxide-semiconductor">complementary metal-oxide-semiconductor</a> (CMOS) technology. The resulting digital image is stored electronically, but can be reproduced on a paper.
</p><p>The camera (or '<a href="/wiki/Camera_obscura" title="Camera obscura">camera obscura</a>') is a dark room or chamber from which, as far as possible, all light is excluded except the light that forms the image. It was discovered and used in the 16th century by painters. The subject being photographed, however, must be illuminated. Cameras can range from small to very large, a whole room that is kept dark while the object to be photographed is in another room where it is properly illuminated. This was common for reproduction photography of flat copy when large film negatives were used (see <a href="/wiki/Process_camera" title="Process camera">Process camera</a>).
</p><p>As soon as photographic materials became "fast" (sensitive) enough for taking <a href="/wiki/Candid_photography" title="Candid photography">candid</a> or surreptitious pictures, small "detective" cameras were made, some actually disguised as a book or handbag or pocket watch (the <i>Ticka</i> camera) or even worn hidden behind an <a href="/wiki/Ascot_tie" title="Ascot tie">Ascot</a> necktie with a tie pin that was really the lens.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Movie_camera" title="Movie camera">movie camera</a> is a type of photographic camera which takes a rapid sequence of photographs on recording medium. In contrast to a still camera, which captures a single snapshot at a time, the movie camera takes a series of images, each called a "frame". This is accomplished through an intermittent mechanism. The frames are later played back in a movie projector at a specific speed, called the "frame rate" (number of frames per second). While viewing, a person's eyes and brain merge the separate pictures to create the illusion of motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stereoscopic">Stereoscopic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Stereoscopic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stereoscopy" title="Stereoscopy">Stereoscopy</a></div>
<p>Photographs, both monochrome and color, can be captured and displayed through two side-by-side images that emulate human stereoscopic vision. Stereoscopic photography was the first that captured figures in motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> While known colloquially as "3-D" photography, the more accurate term is stereoscopy. Such cameras have long been realized by using film and more recently in digital electronic methods (including cell phone cameras).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dualphotography">Dualphotography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Dualphotography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dualphotography" title="Dualphotography">Dualphotography</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1485016840_IMG_7518.JPG_larger.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="428" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/1485016840_IMG_7518.JPG_larger.jpg/220px-1485016840_IMG_7518.JPG_larger.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/1485016840_IMG_7518.JPG_larger.jpg/330px-1485016840_IMG_7518.JPG_larger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/1485016840_IMG_7518.JPG_larger.jpg/440px-1485016840_IMG_7518.JPG_larger.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1485016840_IMG_7518.JPG_larger.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An example of a dualphoto using a smartphone based app</div></div></div>
<p>Dualphotography consists of photographing a scene from both sides of a photographic device at once (e.g. camera for back-to-back dualphotography, or two networked cameras for portal-plane dualphotography). The dualphoto apparatus can be used to simultaneously capture both the subject and the photographer, or both sides of a geographical place at once, thus adding a supplementary narrative layer to that of a single image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Full-spectrum.2C_ultraviolet_and_infrared"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Full-spectrum,_ultraviolet_and_infrared">Full-spectrum, ultraviolet and infrared</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Full-spectrum, ultraviolet and infrared">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Full_spectrum_photography" title="Full spectrum photography">Full spectrum photography</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn%27s_Rings_in_Ultraviolet_Light.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="2150" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Saturn%27s_Rings_in_Ultraviolet_Light.png/220px-Saturn%27s_Rings_in_Ultraviolet_Light.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Saturn%27s_Rings_in_Ultraviolet_Light.png/330px-Saturn%27s_Rings_in_Ultraviolet_Light.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Saturn%27s_Rings_in_Ultraviolet_Light.png/440px-Saturn%27s_Rings_in_Ultraviolet_Light.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn%27s_Rings_in_Ultraviolet_Light.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This image of the <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">rings of Saturn</a> is an example of the application of <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_photography" title="Ultraviolet photography">ultraviolet photography</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_astronomy" title="Ultraviolet astronomy">astronomy</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_photography" title="Ultraviolet photography">Ultraviolet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Infrared_photography" title="Infrared photography">infrared</a> films have been available for many decades and employed in a variety of photographic avenues since the 1960s. New technological trends in digital photography have opened a new direction in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Full_spectrum_photography" title="Full spectrum photography">full spectrum photography</a>, where careful filtering choices across the ultraviolet, visible and infrared lead to new artistic visions.
</p><p>Modified digital cameras can detect some ultraviolet, all of the visible and much of the near infrared spectrum, as most digital imaging sensors are sensitive from about 350 nm to 1000 nm. An off-the-shelf digital camera contains an infrared <a href="/wiki/Hot_mirror" title="Hot mirror">hot mirror</a> filter that blocks most of the infrared and a bit of the ultraviolet that would otherwise be detected by the sensor, narrowing the accepted range from about 400 nm to 700 nm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Replacing a hot mirror or infrared blocking filter with an infrared pass or a wide spectrally transmitting filter allows the camera to detect the wider spectrum light at greater sensitivity. Without the hot-mirror, the red, green and blue (or cyan, yellow and magenta) colored micro-filters placed over the sensor elements pass varying amounts of ultraviolet (blue window) and infrared (primarily red and somewhat lesser the green and blue micro-filters).
</p><p>Uses of full spectrum photography are for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fine_art_photography" title="Fine art photography">fine art photography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing#Geodetic" title="Remote sensing">geology</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_forensic_photography" title="History of forensic photography">forensics</a> and law enforcement.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Layering"><span class="anchor" id="layering"></span>Layering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Layering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Layering is a photographic <a href="/wiki/Composition_(visual_arts)" title="Composition (visual arts)">composition</a> technique that manipulates the foreground, subject or middle-ground, and background layers in a way that they all work together to tell a story through the image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Layers may be incorporated by altering the focal length, distorting the perspective by positioning the camera in a certain spot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> People, movement, light and a variety of objects can be used in layering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Light_field">Light field</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Light field">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light-field_camera" title="Light-field camera">Light-field camera</a></div>
<p>Digital methods of image capture and display processing have enabled the new technology of "light field photography" (also known as synthetic aperture photography). This process allows focusing at various depths of field to be selected <i>after</i> the photograph has been captured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> As explained by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a> in 1846, the "<a href="/wiki/Light_field" title="Light field">light field</a>" is understood as 5-dimensional, with each point in 3-D space having attributes of two more angles that define the direction of each ray passing through that point.
</p><p>These additional vector attributes can be captured optically through the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microlenses" title="Microlenses">microlenses</a> at each pixel point within the 2-dimensional image sensor. Every pixel of the final image is actually a selection from each sub-array located under each microlens, as identified by a post-image capture focus algorithm.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_(Arabidopsis_thaliana)_lehekarv_(trihhoom)_311_0804.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_%28Arabidopsis_thaliana%29_lehekarv_%28trihhoom%29_311_0804.JPG/220px-M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_%28Arabidopsis_thaliana%29_lehekarv_%28trihhoom%29_311_0804.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_%28Arabidopsis_thaliana%29_lehekarv_%28trihhoom%29_311_0804.JPG/330px-M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_%28Arabidopsis_thaliana%29_lehekarv_%28trihhoom%29_311_0804.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_%28Arabidopsis_thaliana%29_lehekarv_%28trihhoom%29_311_0804.JPG/440px-M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_%28Arabidopsis_thaliana%29_lehekarv_%28trihhoom%29_311_0804.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_(Arabidopsis_thaliana)_lehekarv_(trihhoom)_311_0804.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Devices other than cameras can be used to record images. <a href="/wiki/Trichome" title="Trichome">Trichome</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Arabidopsis_thaliana" title="Arabidopsis thaliana">Arabidopsis thaliana</a></i> seen via <a href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscope" title="Scanning electron microscope">scanning electron microscope</a>. Note that image has been <a href="/wiki/Image_editing" title="Image editing">edited</a> by adding colors to clarify structure or to add an aesthetic effect. Heiti Paves from <a href="/wiki/Tallinn_University_of_Technology" title="Tallinn University of Technology">Tallinn University of Technology</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Besides the camera, other methods of forming images with light are available. For instance, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photocopy" title="Photocopy">photocopy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Xerography" title="Xerography">xerography</a> machine forms permanent images but uses the transfer of static <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electrical charges</a> rather than photographic medium, hence the term <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrophotography" title="Electrophotography">electrophotography</a>. <a href="/wiki/Photogram" title="Photogram">Photograms</a> are images produced by the shadows of objects cast on the photographic paper, without the use of a camera. Objects can also be placed directly on the glass of an <a href="/wiki/Image_scanner" title="Image scanner">image scanner</a> to produce digital pictures.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amateur">Amateur</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Amateur">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Amateur photographers take photos for personal use, as a <a href="/wiki/Hobby" title="Hobby">hobby</a> or out of casual interest, rather than as a business or job. The quality of amateur work can be comparable to that of many professionals. Amateurs can fill a gap in subjects or topics that might not otherwise be photographed if they are not commercially useful or salable. Amateur photography grew during the late 19th century due to the popularization of the hand-held camera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Twenty-first century <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">social media</a> and near-ubiquitous <a href="/wiki/Camera_phone" title="Camera phone">camera phones</a> have made photographic and video recording pervasive in everyday life. In the mid-2010s <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphone</a> cameras added numerous automatic assistance features like <a href="/wiki/Color_management" title="Color management">color management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Autofocus" title="Autofocus">autofocus</a> <a href="/wiki/Face_detection" title="Face detection">face detection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Image_stabilization" title="Image stabilization">image stabilization</a> that significantly decreased skill and effort needed to take high quality images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial">Commercial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Commercial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Prose plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-Prose" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOS:LIST" title="MOS:LIST">list</a> format but may read better as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOS:PROSE" title="MOS:PROSE">prose</a></b>.<span
